<div id="review_2752962457" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2752962457" />
    <a title="Kimber Silver" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92166434-kimber-silver"><img alt="Kimber Silver" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547493035p2/92166434.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2752962457?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kimber Silver" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kimber Silver" href="/user/show/92166434-kimber-silver">Kimber Silver</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/92166434-kimber-silver?shelf=must-read">must-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/92166434-kimber-silver?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/92166434-kimber-silver?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2752962457" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6201932496557625735"><i>
  <b> "I know that right choices by definition are the means by which life crystallizes loss." <br/>― Amor Towles, Rules of Civility</b>
</i><br/><br/>Have you ever been immersed in something so enchanting that you have no idea how special it is until it’s over? This is the feeling I’m left with after reading <i>Rules of Civility</i>. Amor Towles’ dreamy lyrical prose is so beautiful that I was transported back in time to a glamorous life of martinis and manners. I could taste the bathtub gin on my tongue as the sultry jazz tw</span>
  <span id="freeText6201932496557625735" style="display:none"><i>
  <b> "I know that right choices by definition are the means by which life crystallizes loss." <br />― Amor Towles, Rules of Civility</b>
</i><br /><br />Have you ever been immersed in something so enchanting that you have no idea how special it is until it’s over? This is the feeling I’m left with after reading <i>Rules of Civility</i>. Amor Towles’ dreamy lyrical prose is so beautiful that I was transported back in time to a glamorous life of martinis and manners. I could taste the bathtub gin on my tongue as the sultry jazz twirled me across the dancefloor and out onto the snowy streets of New York at the stroke of midnight to usher in 1938.<br /><br />Katherine Kontent, a second-generation American of Russian descent, strikes out from Brooklyn to make her way in Manhattan. This is where she and Evelyn Ross, an import from the Midwest, meet and become the best of friends. Katey is a bookworm endowed with a pluckiness that will serve her well. Evey has her heart set on the moon and stars and intends to get them, by hook or by crook. The pair bump into Theodore Grey, or 'Tinker' to his friends, at an out-of-the-way Jazz club on New Year’s Eve. The newly-formed trio subsequently embark on a journey that will change their lives. The women show him a poor girl's version of New York while Tinker holds the golden ticket that will allow them into the world of the privileged classes.<br /><br />Some surprising twists drew me deeper into the fray while the well-developed cast of supporting characters rounded out this delicious tale. <br /><br />I freely admit that this book didn't immediately blow my socks off. I fell for <i>A Gentleman in Moscow</i> and initially found myself comparing the two. Nevertheless, I gave the story a chance to stand on its own and, once I'd found the sweet spot, it was delicious. It’s a rarity to read a book that touched me so profoundly that I cried through its final pages. <br /><br /><i>Rules of Civility</i> ticked all the boxes for me!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6201932496557625735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1069339795" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1069339795" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1069339795?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=literary-stuff">literary-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=new-york-new-york">new-york-new-york</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1069339795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2797068812662382205"><b>$1.99 Kindle sale, August 2, 2019.</b> I’ve wanted to read Amor Towles' first novel, <i>Rules of Civility</i> (named after George Washington's list of 110 social etiquette and proper behavior rules) since I fell in love with his whimsical <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34066798.A_Gentleman_in_Moscow' title='A Gentleman in Moscow by Amor Towles' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Gentleman in Moscow</a>. I didn't like this one as well, but it's still worth reading.<br/><br/><i>Rules of Civility</i> starts off slowly, the story of a young woman making her way in New York City in 1938, but with enough interesting details that I found it absorbing. The main character, </span>
  <span id="freeText2797068812662382205" style="display:none"><b>$1.99 Kindle sale, August 2, 2019.</b> I’ve wanted to read Amor Towles' first novel, <i>Rules of Civility</i> (named after George Washington's list of 110 social etiquette and proper behavior rules) since I fell in love with his whimsical <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34066798.A_Gentleman_in_Moscow" title="A Gentleman in Moscow by Amor Towles" rel="nofollow noopener">A Gentleman in Moscow</a>. I didn't like this one as well, but it's still worth reading.<br /><br /><i>Rules of Civility</i> starts off slowly, the story of a young woman making her way in New York City in 1938, but with enough interesting details that I found it absorbing. The main character, Brooklyn native Katey Kontent (a made-up name; she's hiding from her own past and heritage), along with her friend Eve, meet a handsome young banker, Tinker Grey. They all hit it off and start hanging out together, with some romantic tension between them: Eve called dibs on Tinker, but he and Katey have a deeper connection. When an accident occurs that severely injures Eve, Tinker makes a choice, and Katey starts to make some life-changing career choices and changes, battling social barriers along the way. <br /><br />The plot meanders through most of the year 1938, and I kind of felt like I was just powering through it, but then it picks up pace and interest toward the end, when we start to find out who people really are underneath the masks they wear. The ending really packs a punch. <br /><br />A story of selling out, bad choices and good ones, finding yourself, and even redemption.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2797068812662382205" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2363079599" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2363079599" />
    <a title="Erin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17957823-erin"><img alt="Erin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597778686p2/17957823.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2363079599?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin" href="/user/show/17957823-erin">Erin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17957823-erin?shelf=library-borrowed">library-borrowed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2363079599" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5186233354384458248">Well, that debut novel was*ahem* interesting. A tale of three twentysomethings in New York in the 1930's managed to give this book a very "F.Scott Fitzgerald " vibe, but I just wasn't wooed enough to climb as high on the rating scale. If this was bookclub, I would probably be slinging bag G&T's like there was no tomorrow while all these 4 and 5 stars would be waxing on about this coming of age story with its beautiful prose. But the only things I walked away from this book is a reading list(some</span>
  <span id="freeText5186233354384458248" style="display:none">Well, that debut novel was*ahem* interesting. A tale of three twentysomethings in New York in the 1930's managed to give this book a very "F.Scott Fitzgerald " vibe, but I just wasn't wooed enough to climb as high on the rating scale. If this was bookclub, I would probably be slinging bag G&amp;T's like there was no tomorrow while all these 4 and 5 stars would be waxing on about this coming of age story with its beautiful prose. But the only things I walked away from this book is a reading list(some fantastic authors are nudged in this one) and relief that I didn't spend any money on this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5186233354384458248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781101517062" href="/book/show/11523279-rules-of-civility">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=american-author">american-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=american-novel">american-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2136327927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12335498516902678817">I don't want to say a lot about this book. I'm a bit tired this morning. Wanted to finish this book and denied myself a few hours of sleep.<br/><br/>This is the story of Kate, Eve and Tinker in the New York of 1938, where it was possible to climb the social ladder with a few rules from the father of the American republic's, <i>George Washington's Rules of Civility and Decent Behavior</i>; a few well-positioned social connections; and and a whiff of intelligence. Everybody had a chance if you knew the rules. <br/><br/>In 1</span>
  <span id="freeText12335498516902678817" style="display:none">I don't want to say a lot about this book. I'm a bit tired this morning. Wanted to finish this book and denied myself a few hours of sleep.<br /><br />This is the story of Kate, Eve and Tinker in the New York of 1938, where it was possible to climb the social ladder with a few rules from the father of the American republic's, <i>George Washington's Rules of Civility and Decent Behavior</i>; a few well-positioned social connections; and and a whiff of intelligence. Everybody had a chance if you knew the rules. <br /><br />In 1966 Kate is reflecting on that year's experiences which changed their lives forever. And all through the prose a love letter to New York is written in embossed gold. <br /><br />A wonderful read. My first encounter with this author was his book <i>A Gentleman In Moscow</i> which will forever be one of my favorite books. This one comes in at a close second. <br /><br />Quote I want to remember:<blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>The metaphor was apt. A million years of social behavior had worn away this chasm and now you had to pack a mule to get to the bottom of it.</b>
  </i>
</blockquote> Washingtonia left a lot to learn and a lot to regret in the case of these three young adults who learnt more about themselves and life in a year than most people in a lifetime. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>For Tinker Grey, this little book wasn’t a series of moral aspirations—it was a primer on social advancement. A do-it-yourself charm school. A sort of How to Win Friends and Influence People 150 years ahead of its time.</b>
  </i>
</blockquote> In the end it was about choices. Between Anne Grandyn on the one hand and Wallace Wolcott on the other, there was a midway waiting to be discovered.<br /><br />For me this novel was something between Judith Krantz's <i> Princess Daisy</i> - lite and Hemingway's nostalgia, with a dash of philosophy added. Whatever it was, it worked for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12335498516902678817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1741548165?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PorshaJo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PorshaJo" href="/user/show/5464046-porshajo">PorshaJo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads">challengereads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads-2016">challengereads-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1741548165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5771688192799700961">Rating 3.5<br/><br/>There is a movie by Woody Allen, Midnight in Paris (awesome movie), that many say 'its a love letter to Paris'. A love letter to a particular time in history, the roaring 20s, where many literary and artistic people socialized. The Rules of Civility, I felt, was Towles love affair. His love affair with New York city, his love affair with the late 30s, and his love of literature.<br/><br/>The story follows Katey Kontent (really?) who is twenty five, living in New York's Greenwich Village, moving </span>
  <span id="freeText5771688192799700961" style="display:none">Rating 3.5<br /><br />There is a movie by Woody Allen, Midnight in Paris (awesome movie), that many say 'its a love letter to Paris'. A love letter to a particular time in history, the roaring 20s, where many literary and artistic people socialized. The Rules of Civility, I felt, was Towles love affair. His love affair with New York city, his love affair with the late 30s, and his love of literature.<br /><br />The story follows Katey Kontent (really?) who is twenty five, living in New York's Greenwich Village, moving along and socializing with various types in the late 30's. In the blurb, it also talks about Tinker Grey and her friend Eve and that fateful evening. But I did not think they were central to the story. I wanted to hear more of this trio but the story focuses on Katey, and all those people that cross her path. Yes, many of whom she would have never met, had it not been for that meeting with Tinker Grey. <br /><br />I liked the story, but wanted to like it more. Not too much really happens with Katey, other than meeting some rich folks, socializing, and trying to get ahead in life, all while trying to maintain a sense of elegance. At first, I loved all the literary references, but then it became too much. There is no doubt that Towles can write, and write vivid descriptions of times and places. I look forward to reading more from him.<br /><br />I listened to this one via audio and it was great. The narrator is one that I have heard before and she did not disappoint. Overall, I'm glad I have finally read this one, but doubt it will stick with me.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5771688192799700961" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3009557118?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3009557118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16184771229793400942"><pre> ‘Friends are the envy of angels.’<br/> ‘The decisions we have just made will shape our lives for decades to come.’ – Rules of Civility, Amor Towles</pre><br/><br/>Set in New York City of the 1930s, <b>Rules of Civility</b> followed the destiny of a group of young people in their twenties who greeted life with enthusiasm and hope. NYC was perceived to be a haven of possibilities where individuals could re-invent themselves simply by altering their names. Towles’ love of this city could be felt in his depiction of its vibra</span>
  <span id="freeText16184771229793400942" style="display:none"><pre> ‘Friends are the envy of angels.’<br /> ‘The decisions we have just made will shape our lives for decades to come.’ – Rules of Civility, Amor Towles</pre><br /><br />Set in New York City of the 1930s, <b>Rules of Civility</b> followed the destiny of a group of young people in their twenties who greeted life with enthusiasm and hope. NYC was perceived to be a haven of possibilities where individuals could re-invent themselves simply by altering their names. Towles’ love of this city could be felt in his depiction of its vibrancy, the contrast between the rich in Central Park West and the poorer majority living in shoebox apartments, the glitzy hotels that offer fine dining alongside humble diners and neighborhood cafes. It is easy to fall in love with NYC and believe one can shape one’s destiny.<br /><br /><b>Rules of Civility</b> is the story of Katya Kontent, an intelligent, 25-year-old, second generation American of Russian origin (renamed Katey) and her relationship with four colorful contemporaries who dominated her life in 1938: Eve Ross (Evelyn), her ravishing roommate; Theodore Grey (Tinker), a debonair banker and the love of her life; Wallace Wolcott and Dicky Vanderwhile, Katey’s big-hearted romantic interests, the former a shy multimillionaire ‘grander than Mount Rushmore’; the latter rich but sweet and unspoilt. <br /><br /> Katey’s conundrum begins on New Year’s eve of 1937 at Hotspot, a jazz bar when she and Eve are first attracted to Tinker, tall with brown hair and blue eyes. Sharing clothes is easier than negotiating a rivalry for Tinker’s affection. Unlike Katey who is sensitive, calm, and thoughtful, Eve is spunky, forward, and opportunistic. Tinker, on the other hand, put up a credible show of impartiality when the threesome are checking out theatres and bar hopping. Between the girls, Tinker, perceived to have deep pockets, is called ‘dibs’ (a purveyor of the glitzy life denied two working girls). Then an outing on a snowy evening set in motion a series of decisions that changed the relationship amongst the trio. Looking back at 1938 in 1966, Katey recalls, ‘It wasn’t about who had dibs now or who was sitting next to whom in the cinema. The game has changed; or rather, it wasn’t a game at all anymore.’ <br /><br />I marveled at Towles’ penetrating insight into the female psyche. The story is a believable first-person narration by Katey, a daring venture by a male author. How did he fathom how a woman might feel? Apart from Katey, there is also Anne Grandyn, a sophisticated businesswoman with impeccable taste who claims to be Tinker’s godmother. Towle masterfully revealed the subtle undercurrents of admiration, envy, and contempt. <br /><br />I also marveled at Towles’ skill at calibrating surprises in this novel. An unexpected plot development makes the reader take a second look at Tinker Grey, the embodiment of the Manhattan success story. Who is he really behind the cashmere coat, Windsor knot, and gold cigarette lighter? I changed my opinions a few times about Tinker because Towles unraveled his personality a little at a time. He allowed us to piece together circumstantial details that stitched together a full portrait of this man loved by so many women. In fact, when the last page was turned, I retraced my steps, this time re-observing details I had missed, casual remarks, shifts in mood, and social dynamics. On second reading, Towles’ artistry becomes plain as day. <br /><br />I love how Towles made reading an engaging pursuit for a couple of his characters. The title of this novel comes from a slender book of Washingtonia titled ‘The Rules of Civility &amp; Decent Behaviour’ by George Washington that Tinker read in his youth. It was a gift from his mother on his 14th birthday, a guide on how to win friends and influence people. Additionally, Katey is a reader who drowns her sorrows in books. It is wonderful hearing her muse about Charles Dickens and Agatha Christie. In one of her saddest days, Katey confessed, <i>‘In retrospect, my cup of coffee bas been the works of Charles Dickens. Admittedly, there’s something a little annoying about all those plucky underprivileged kids and the aptly named agents of villainy. But I’ve come to realize that however blue my circumstances, if after finishing a chapter of a Dickens novel I feel a miss-my-stop-on-the-train sort of compulsion to read on, then everything is probably going to be just fine.’</i> I understood that. Katey found Christie’s murder mysteries to be ‘tremendously satisfying’ because ‘in Christie’s universe everyone eventually gets what they deserve.’ This, too, I know to be true.<br /><br /><b>Rules of Civility</b> is a sad story with a fair dose of realism. The dominant emotion I felt was loss. Loss comes in the form of unrealized possibilities. I mourn the loss of what could have been under a different set of circumstances. Dicky, one of my favorite characters, remarked to Katey, <i>‘If we only fell in love with people who were perfect for us,…then there wouldn’t be so much fuss about love in the first place.’</i> Losing those we love is tortuous. Yet, when the story ends, one is left with a sense of relief that life goes on in the absence of perfect love. We make the best choices we know, and the <i>’right choices by definition are the means by which life crystallizes loss.’</i><br /><br /><b>Rules of Civility</b> is Amor Towles’ first book. Already one sees the makings of an excellent raconteur. It is dwarfed by <b>‘A Gentleman in Moscow’ </b> but it should be appreciated for its own merits.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16184771229793400942" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_314555592" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/314555592" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/314555592?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="MP3 CD 9781101526361" href="/book/show/12578076-rules-of-civility">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=contemp-novel">contemp-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=nyc">nyc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer314555592" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5664668182642431860">In summary, I loved listening to this audiobook. Why? First of all, this book is a must for anyone who loves NYC. Secondly, almost every line refers to places and books and artists. There is a wonderful message. The author is a master of metaphor. Most every sentence implies more than the bare words. One example: Katey pronounces her surname Kon-TENT. Don't you see the difference between that and KON-tent? Think about it. The plot throws you a looper. The characters become real people .In the be</span>
  <span id="freeText5664668182642431860" style="display:none">In summary, I loved listening to this audiobook. Why? First of all, this book is a must for anyone who loves NYC. Secondly, almost every line refers to places and books and artists. There is a wonderful message. The author is a master of metaphor. Most every sentence implies more than the bare words. One example: Katey pronounces her surname Kon-TENT. Don't you see the difference between that and KON-tent? Think about it. The plot throws you a looper. The characters become real people .In the beginning I wondered if the different characters' actions were believable. Yes, they most certainly were. People are complicated. As with real people it takes a while to understand these characters. By the end of the novel each one is very special, each in their own way. And finally there is subtle, sophisticated humor. Absolutely excellent narration by Rebecca Lowman. <br /><br />The title of this book refers to George Washington's penmanship assignment entitled "Rules of Civility &amp; Decent Behavior in Company and Conversation". Are you curious for more? Look here: (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.foundationsmag.com/civility.html">http://www.foundationsmag.com/civilit...</a>) These 110 rules are listed at the end of the book, but I did not read them.... The title is very apt, for this book is all about to what purpose these rules should be followed. This book is all about the choices each of us makes in determining our fate, because we do make choices. Our fate is not predestined. <br /><br />******************<br /><br />Oh, what I wouldn't do to go shopping at Bendel's. I can dream it still exists and is still the same?<br /><br />****************<br /><br />Through Chapter 9:<br /><br />Why have I not grabbed this book sooner?! It is not just good, it is really good. This book is for those people who demand books that are well written. Exciting plots are not enough. You know there are those books that are fun because the plot keeps you hanging on. Then there are those books which are fabulous because every line has you thinking, not about the plot, but about what is described, the place, the people, or how a character is responding, or how would I react in that situation. I am not saying the plot doesn't move forward, I am merely saying what happens isn't that necessary because the writing in itself keeps you thinking. I love the writing.<br /><br />So what is it I love? I wrote below that the author superbly describes NYC. By that I do not mean what you see, but what you feel when you are there in that place. There is a static electricity in the air that is NYC. There is the honking of the cabs, there are the dark summer nights, there is the hot, sticky air in the summer. These things that I mention here are NOT described in this book, but this author has found all the other characteristics of this city that are so hard to capture. <br /><br />I thought this was a nostalgia trip for me...... but it is more than that. Amor Towles describes places and events that I have never experienced, and I feel I am there too! An example, the Belmont race-tracks. Katey is there at 5 A.M. on a weekday. There you find the down and out, those who bet with their last dime, the wealthy, those who own horses and have a stake in the outcomes, the trainers... Each sit in their own places. Each is described with similes so that you see in which group each one belongs. How they hold their bodies, how they move, where they sit, how they are clothed, what they are eating and drinking - all of this you see. You feel the excitement. This is NOT done through boring descriptions. It is done through wonderful similes and metaphors so you understand and know immediately how a person almost feels because you recognize the visions conjured by the metaphors. This book is filled with the words"like" and "as if". It is filled with gorgeous metaphors that you snap up immediately. That is the author's trick..... But this is no easy trick. How has the author known how to capture those hunched shoulders? Where else is that shoulder held with that hunch so that readers will recognize it immediately? Then Towles puts it there for us in his metaphor. <br /><br />Back to the race tracks. They are referred to as "Race-Arounds". What a wonderful expression. There are hunched backs, then straightened backs released as if on a spring when the horses surprise. Hands grip cups where "the absence of steam said the cup was filled with liquor". You smell the paddocks. And here, as elsewhere, what you see is compared to the lines of famous literature. It was like "circles of Dante's Inferno". Over and over again there are reference to artists and authors and photographers and books you have read. You remember these books or that painting and you know exactly what is expressed. This is a book for the literate, the well-educated. It is certainly for the well-read. It not only captures the jazz clubs of the 30s, but also art trends, the secretarial girls, literature and even butterfly collections! Eve is compared to the portraits of John Singer Sargent. Doesn't that draw a picture for you?<br /><br />Another thought.... How has this author so well captured the essence of his female characters? Is there some women here on the sidelines?<br /><br />So, I am terribly enjoying this book for <u>
  <b>how</b>
</u> it us written. The events are rather insignificant for me; sometimes I do wonder would people make such decisions? Would it happen like that? But I have only read nine chapters. Maybe I will change my mind about that too. I am beginning to figure out who these people are: Tinker and Katey and Eve. We are talking 1938, NYC. Tinker is from the well-to-do. Eve and Katey are scratching the dirt, but Eve is never one to be under another's thumb, as she clearly states at the very beginning. One is from Brooklyn with Russian immigrant parents.That is Katey. The other is from the Midwest. What is intriguing is that I am sure there is more to understand about these three disparate figures. Who is the social climber? Katey is the erudite blue-collar worker, a stenographer at a law firm, but under the exterior who is she? How are they similar? How do they differ....... and this is a friendship of three! That never works. Who really loves who? <br /><br />I like the narration by Rebecca Lowman. Her voice is a perfect balance to the struggles of the the three. There are struggles. <br /><br />************************<br /><br />Well, I definitely love the way NYC is described. I lived there. It has a special feel to it. You go into that city and you feel it in the air. You feel <u>that</u> when you read this book. It makes me terribly nostalgic. There are so many details that perfectly capture the atmosphere of the place - the cabs, the sounds, Greenwich Village, the restaurants, the food. Each aspect is perfectly depicted with a simile that strikes home perfectly. Gosh, I am amazed. I was so worried when I started this book....</span>
  <a data-text-id="5664668182642431860" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9228925656940718685"><i>—Oh stop, Eve said. It’s dreadful. What is it?<br/>—Virginia Woolf.<br/>—Ugh. Tinker brought home all these novels by women as if that’s what I needed to get me back on my feet. He’s surrounded my bed with them. It’s as if he’s planning to brick me in. Isn’t there anything else?<br/></i> <br/>Rules of Civility left me cold. I did not hate it, I did not like, I certainly did not love it as much as other people, including a lot of readers whose reviews I value, loved this book.<br/> <br/>I don't even know whether it was the detach</span>
  <span id="freeText9228925656940718685" style="display:none"><i>—Oh stop, Eve said. It’s dreadful. What is it?<br />—Virginia Woolf.<br />—Ugh. Tinker brought home all these novels by women as if that’s what I needed to get me back on my feet. He’s surrounded my bed with them. It’s as if he’s planning to brick me in. Isn’t there anything else?<br /></i> <br />Rules of Civility left me cold. I did not hate it, I did not like, I certainly did not love it as much as other people, including a lot of readers whose reviews I value, loved this book.<br /> <br />I don't even know whether it was the detached voice of Katey Kontent that made me feel nothing about anything in this book or whether it was the embellished detail of 1930s jazziness that got on my nerves and made me look hard for another aspect of the book that I could get into. Something like a plot or an interesting character. Or at least one that did not feel like a cardboard cut-out.<br />I may have detested F.S. Fitzgerald's main characters, but at least they were memorable. I may have disliked Evelyn Waugh's tone and snobbishness, but at least his books carried an air of authenticity by attempting to be satire.<br /> <br />I don't even know what the book was trying to do.<br /> <br />Nope, Rules of Civility just did not work for me.</span>

<div id="review_2936674503" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2936674503" />
    <a title="Yun" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30181442-yun"><img alt="Yun" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497486100p2/30181442.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2936674503?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yun" href="/user/show/30181442-yun">Yun</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2936674503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7054733537217710466">I really enjoyed <i>Rules of Civility</i>, but looking back on it, it's hard to explain exactly why. On the surface, there's nothing terribly special about the story itself, which centers around Katey, a working class girl living her life in Manhattan during the 1930s. A chance encounter propels her to friendships with wealthy Manhattanites, which provide her with opportunity and privilege as well as relationships and experiences that shape who she is.<br/><br/>And yet, there is something so compelling about thi</span>
  <span id="freeText7054733537217710466" style="display:none">I really enjoyed <i>Rules of Civility</i>, but looking back on it, it's hard to explain exactly why. On the surface, there's nothing terribly special about the story itself, which centers around Katey, a working class girl living her life in Manhattan during the 1930s. A chance encounter propels her to friendships with wealthy Manhattanites, which provide her with opportunity and privilege as well as relationships and experiences that shape who she is.<br /><br />And yet, there is something so compelling about this story, and it must be Towles's writing. He brings so much charm, insight, and longing into it, deftly exploring the topics of money, independence, choices, friendship, and love. His characters are interesting, if a tad emotionless. They seem not to be bothered by anything, which is a little underplayed, but maybe that cool aloofness adds to the charm and atmosphere of this tale.<br /><br />Towles takes what would be an otherwise pedestrian story and turns it into something special with his sharp writing. At this point, I'm two for two when it come to Towles, so I'm going to keep reading anything he writes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7054733537217710466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer248132526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer554504186674762848">I enjoy character-driven novels. This one is made perfect by focusing on a specific time and place: 1938 in NYC. It's a year between the Great Depression and the beginning of WWII. Even the poised, reflective characters are carefree enough to hang out and drink, listen to jazz and have madcap adventures. Fun to eavesdrop on all that. <br/><br/>There's a wonderful device used to demonstrate one person's character. At the beginning of the book, our narrator finds Tinker Grey's picture twice in a photographe</span>
  <span id="freeText554504186674762848" style="display:none">I enjoy character-driven novels. This one is made perfect by focusing on a specific time and place: 1938 in NYC. It's a year between the Great Depression and the beginning of WWII. Even the poised, reflective characters are carefree enough to hang out and drink, listen to jazz and have madcap adventures. Fun to eavesdrop on all that. <br /><br />There's a wonderful device used to demonstrate one person's character. At the beginning of the book, our narrator finds Tinker Grey's picture twice in a photographer's exhibit. Tinker also appears twice in a class picture.<br /><br />I like the non-preachy examination of wealth, class and ambition in America.<br /><br />Second reading for DCL book group. </span>
  <a data-text-id="554504186674762848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1MfqP5eiLdTK1ZHq1ziiCQhgEG7B53ECe5Svl9cOIWK8RFI9iBaCLZcal04TJKkcCFcj3NC0shjYwBkq//Gqzw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('afI5ycEU/QvVVtiVFUJJQcU//K1Zo2aMJK4WVt/BbnQBcYHL3qBS8oiZ3jHRXkJUxQjPH0jwpZaH+qDr9z7l2Q==')})
  }
//]]>

Ultimately, though, it leads her to a new understanding of the kind of life she craves - and the kind of freedom it takes to pursue it. It will also lead to the love of her life, a love that stands out from all the rest.\n\nNow ninety-five years old and telling her story at last, Vivian recalls how the events of those years altered the course of her life - and the gusto and autonomy with which she approached it. &quot;At some point in a woman\'s life, she just gets tired of being ashamed all the time,&quot; she muses. A born naturalist with just one day of school, she takes life\'s lessons from the land, learning the real ways of the world from the dishonest signals of fireflies. But while she has the skills to live in solitude forever, the time comes when she yearns to be touched and loved. Drawn to two young men from town, who are each intrigued by her wild beauty, Kya opens herself to a new and startling world–until the unthinkable happens.\n\nIn Where the Crawdads Sing, Owens juxtaposes an exquisite ode to the natural world against a profound coming of age story and haunting mystery. Thought-provoking, wise, and deeply moving, Owens’s debut novel reminds us that we are forever shaped by the child within us, while also subject to the beautiful and violent secrets that nature keeps.\n\nThe story asks how isolation influences the behavior of a young woman, who like all of us, has the genetic propensity to belong to a group.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4536964.Amor_Towles">About Amor Towles</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4536964.Amor_Towles"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1472937967p3/4536964.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4536964.Amor_Towles">Amor Towles

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2223236368650111913">Born and raised in the Boston area, Amor Towles graduated from Yale College and received an MA in English from Stanford University. Having worked as an investment professional in Manhattan for over twenty years, he now devotes himself fulltime to writing. His first novel, Rules of Civility, published in 2011, was a New York Times bestseller in both hardcover and paperback and was ranked by the Wal</span>
  <span id="freeText2223236368650111913" style="display:none">Born and raised in the Boston area, Amor Towles graduated from Yale College and received an MA in English from Stanford University. Having worked as an investment professional in Manhattan for over twenty years, he now devotes himself fulltime to writing. His first novel, Rules of Civility, published in 2011, was a New York Times bestseller in both hardcover and paperback and was ranked by the Wall Street Journal as one of the best books of 2011. The book was optioned by Lionsgate to be made into a feature film and its French translation received the 2012 Prix Fitzgerald. His second novel, A Gentleman in Moscow, published in 2016, was also a New York Times bestseller and was ranked as one of the best books of 2016 by the Chicago Tribune, the Miami Herald, the Philadelphia Inquirer, the St. Louis Dispatch, and NPR. Both novels have been translated into over fifteen languages. <br /><br />Mr. Towles, who lives in Manhattan with his wife and two children, is an ardent fan of early 20th century painting, 1950’s jazz, 1970’s cop shows, rock &amp; roll on vinyl, obsolete accessories, manifestoes, breakfast pastries, pasta, liquor, snow-days, Tuscany, Provence, Disneyland, Hollywood, the cast of Casablanca, 007, Captain Kirk, Bob Dylan (early, mid, and late phases), the wee hours, card games, cafés, and the cookies made by both of his grandmothers.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2223236368650111913" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/14950407-rules-of-civility">Quotes from Rules of Civility</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;In our twenties, when there is still so much time ahead of us, time that seems ample for a hundred indecisions, for a hundred visions and revisions—we draw a card, and we must decide right then and there whether to keep that card and discard the next, or discard the first card and keep the second. And before we know it, the deck has been played out and the decisions we have just made will shape our lives for decades to come.&rdquo;</span>
